##### You said:

Thanks. Tell me about the railway strikes in the US in the 1870s

###### ChatGPT said:

The **railroad strikes of the 1870s**, particularly the **Great Railroad Strike of 1877**, were among the first major **nationwide labor uprisings** in the U.S. They weren’t explicitly anarchist-led, but they reflected deep **working-class anger against industrial capitalism** and set the stage for later radical labor movements, including anarchist and syndicalist organizing in the U.S.

---

### **The Great Railroad Strike of 1877**

- **Trigger:** The strike began in **July 1877** when the **Baltimore & Ohio Railroad (B&O)** cut workers’ wages for the second time that year, amid an ongoing economic depression.
    
- **Spread:** What started as a **localized strike in West Virginia** quickly spread **nationwide**, with railroad workers in **Pennsylvania, Ohio, Maryland, Illinois, Missouri, and beyond** walking off the job.
    
- **Worker Tactics:**
    
    - Strikers **seized rail yards, blocked trains, and sabotaged equipment**.
        
    - They gained **broad public support**, with some cities experiencing **mass uprisings** beyond just rail workers.
        
    - In places like Pittsburgh, strikers **set fire to railcars and fought police and militia forces**.
        

---

### **Government & Corporate Repression**

- **Militarized Crackdowns:** Local police forces were quickly overwhelmed, so **state militias and federal troops were deployed** to crush the strikes.
    
- **Massacres:**
    
    - **Pittsburgh:** National Guard **opened fire on unarmed strikers and civilians**, killing at least **20 people**. In retaliation, workers torched railroad buildings and destroyed equipment.
        
    - **Chicago, Baltimore, and St. Louis:** Similar violent clashes occurred, with **dozens of strikers killed**.
        
- **Strike Suppressed:** By **August 1877**, the uprising had been violently crushed, with over **100 workers killed** nationwide.
    

---

### **Why It Matters**

1. **First Nationwide Strike:** This was **one of the first labor uprisings in U.S. history** that truly **shut down national infrastructure**.
    
2. **Exposed Class Warfare:** It revealed that **the state would side with capitalists** against workers, using **military force** to crush strikes.
    
3. **Radicalized Labor Movements:** The brutal repression fueled the rise of **more militant labor organizing**, including the **Knights of Labor (1869–1890s)**, **the anarchist labor movement**, and **early socialist groups**.
    
4. **Foreshadowed Later Revolts:** The tactics of sabotage, mass mobilization, and direct confrontation foreshadowed later **industrial strikes**, including the **Haymarket Affair (1886)** and the **Pullman Strike (1894)**.
    

---

Would you like a comparison between **the 1877 strikes and later anarchist-influenced labor actions**, or do you want to focus more on the immediate impact this had on U.S. labor organizing?

##### You said:

On your second point in “why it matters” can you elaborate? Was that really the first time in the US where that alignment was exposed?

###### ChatGPT said:

That’s a great question. **The alignment between the state and capital** wasn’t new in 1877, but **this was one of the first large-scale, nationwide moments where it became undeniably clear to a mass working-class audience** that the U.S. government and corporate interests were fundamentally intertwined.

Here’s how **state repression of labor uprisings evolved before and during the 1877 strikes**, and why this particular event was a turning point.

---

### **1. Pre-1877: Early Examples of State-Capital Alignment**

While 1877 wasn’t the **first** time the government had sided with business interests, most earlier instances were **localized, less coordinated, and framed as legal disputes rather than class war.**

#### **A. Anti-Worker Laws & Legal Repression**

- **Commonwealth v. Pullis (1806)** – One of the first court cases to rule that worker strikes were **illegal conspiracies**. Courts routinely ruled against labor actions before 1877, but enforcement was uneven.
    
- **Criminal Conspiracy Laws (1830s–40s)** – Courts frequently broke up **early trade unions**, treating them as illegal organizations.
    

#### **B. Military Force Used Against Workers & Farmers**

- **The Anti-Rent War (1839–1845, New York)** – Landlords used **state militia forces** to crush tenant uprisings against feudal-style rent obligations.
    
- **The Molly Maguires (1860s–1870s, Pennsylvania)** – A secret organization of Irish mine workers was violently repressed by **private detectives (the Pinkertons) and state forces**, culminating in **mass executions (1877)**.
    

These events **showed a pattern**, but they were **mostly local or sector-specific**, without national-scale coordination.

---

### **2. 1877: The Railroad Strike as a Turning Point**

What made the **Great Railroad Strike of 1877** different was its **scale, visibility, and direct military repression**:

✅ **First Time Federal Troops Were Used Against Strikers in Peacetime**

- Prior to 1877, federal troops had been **used primarily to suppress Indigenous resistance and enforce slavery laws.**
    
- In **1877, President Rutherford B. Hayes sent federal troops** to crush the strikes in **multiple states**, signaling that the federal government would intervene **directly on behalf of corporate interests**.
    

✅ **Coordinated State-Military-Corporate Response**

- Unlike earlier, more fragmented interventions, **railroad executives, local police, state militias, and federal troops coordinated directly** to suppress strikes.
    
- The use of **National Guard and federal troops across multiple states** showed a **new level of systematic state-corporate collaboration**.
    

✅ **Mass Worker Radicalization**

- After the massacres in **Pittsburgh, Chicago, and St. Louis**, workers realized that **the state wasn’t just breaking up strikes—it was waging war on them.**
    
- This **shattered illusions** that the government was a neutral force in labor disputes and led to **a surge in anarchist, socialist, and syndicalist organizing.**
    

---

### **3. The Long-Term Consequences**

- **Increased Surveillance & Anti-Labor Laws**
    
    - After 1877, more cities **built police forces explicitly to control labor unrest**.
        
    - Strikebreakers like the **Pinkerton Detective Agency** became a **formal paramilitary force for corporations**.
        
    - **Sedition laws** were expanded to crack down on radical labor organizing.
        
- **The Birth of Organized Radical Labor**
    
    - Groups like the **Knights of Labor (1869–1890s)** gained momentum, pushing for a more organized worker resistance.
        
    - **The Haymarket Affair (1886)** and **Pullman Strike (1894)** directly descended from the lessons of 1877.
        
- **Tactics Shifted Toward Mass Worker Power**
    
    - Seeing the limits of **spontaneous uprisings**, radicals turned to **general strikes, union-based militancy, and mutual aid networks** to resist corporate and state power.
        

---

### **Final Thought:**

While **earlier events had revealed state-capital alignment in specific ways**, **1877 was the moment when working-class people across the U.S. saw—often for the first time—that the government and business elites were actively conspiring to crush labor uprisings with deadly force**.

It wasn’t the **first-ever** instance of this dynamic, but it **was the first time it happened at a national scale**, affecting workers across industries and making **anti-capitalist radicalization a mass phenomenon in the U.S.**

Does this distinction make sense in terms of what you were asking?

##### You said:

Wow, really interesting. I had heard of Pinkerton before but didn’t have the historical context.

###### ChatGPT said:

Yeah, the **Pinkertons** have a long and infamous history—they weren’t just private detectives, but **one of the first organized paramilitary forces used by corporations against workers**. Their role in **crushing labor movements** throughout the late 19th and early 20th centuries is one of the most blatant examples of **private capital using force to suppress worker resistance**.

---

### **Who Were the Pinkertons?**

- Founded in **1850** by **Allan Pinkerton**, initially as a **private detective agency**.
    
- Gained fame for **protecting Abraham Lincoln from an assassination plot in 1861** (before he took office).
    
- After the Civil War, **they shifted from detective work to violently suppressing labor uprisings**.
    

---

### **Pinkertons vs. Labor Movements**

They became infamous for **infiltrating, spying on, and violently attacking striking workers** on behalf of **corporations and railroad tycoons**.

#### **1. The Great Railroad Strike of 1877**

- Hired by **railroad companies** to infiltrate **worker meetings** and act as **strikebreakers**.
    
- Assisted **police and federal troops** in violently putting down protests.
    

#### **2. The Homestead Strike (1892) – Most Infamous Pinkerton Battle**

- **Andrew Carnegie & Henry Clay Frick** hired **300 Pinkerton agents** to break a **steelworkers’ strike** in **Homestead, Pennsylvania**.
    
- Pinkertons arrived **on barges with rifles and machine guns**, attempting to forcibly reopen the factory.
    
- **Workers fought back**, leading to a **full-scale battle on the riverbanks**—**9 strikers and 7 Pinkertons were killed**.
    
- Pennsylvania's **National Guard was eventually called in**, and the **strike was crushed**.
    

#### **3. The Pullman Strike (1894)**

- Workers at the **Pullman Company** went on strike due to wage cuts and high rents in company-owned housing.
    
- Pinkertons were **brought in to break up the strike**, attacking workers and inciting violence.
    
- The federal government then deployed **U.S. troops, killing over 30 workers**.
    

#### **4. Infiltration & Surveillance of Radical Groups (Late 1800s–Early 1900s)**

- Pinkertons **infiltrated anarchist, socialist, and union movements**, feeding intelligence to both **corporations and the federal government**.
    
- **Emma Goldman and the IWW ("Wobblies") were prime targets** of Pinkerton surveillance.
    
- **False flag operations** – Some reports suggest they **planted provocateurs in radical groups** to incite violence, giving authorities justification to crack down.
    

---

### **Legacy & The “Anti-Pinkerton Act”**

- **Public backlash grew** as stories of **Pinkerton brutality** spread.
    
- In **1893, the U.S. government passed the **"Anti-Pinkerton Act,"** barring the federal government from hiring private paramilitary agencies like them.
    
- However, **corporations continued using them**, and their legacy lived on in **modern private security firms**.
    

---

### **Pinkertons Today?**

- Believe it or not, **Pinkerton still exists** as **a corporate security firm** (now owned by the Swedish company Securitas).
    
- They provide **"risk assessment" services**—basically corporate security, espionage, and asset protection.
    
- They’ve even **been linked to modern union-busting efforts**, continuing their long history of suppressing labor movements.
    

---

### **Final Thought**

The **Pinkertons symbolize the extreme lengths capitalism will go to protect itself**—not just through laws and economics, but through **literal violence**. Their role in **turning labor disputes into armed conflicts** is a major reason why anarchists, socialists, and radical labor movements saw capitalism as fundamentally backed by force.

Does this historical context shift how you view the **relationship between private power and state violence** in the U.S.?

##### You said:

Definitely doesn’t shift it, it affirms it. What mass executions happened as a result of the Molly maguires? And was the anti-Pinkerton act actually enforced?

###### ChatGPT said:

Glad to hear it affirms what you already suspected! Now, let’s break down the **Molly Maguires executions** and whether the **Anti-Pinkerton Act** was actually enforced.

---

### **1. The Molly Maguires & the Mass Executions (1877–1879)**

#### **Who Were the Molly Maguires?**

- The **Molly Maguires** were an **underground organization of Irish immigrant coal miners** in Pennsylvania who **fought against brutal labor conditions** in the 1860s and 1870s.
    
- They were **mostly active in the anthracite coal mines**, where they organized **violent resistance against mine owners, police, and strikebreakers**.
    
- They **allegedly carried out assassinations and sabotage** against company officials and informants.
    

#### **The Pinkerton Infiltration & Frame-Up**

- The **Reading Railroad & mine owners hired the Pinkerton Detective Agency** to destroy the Molly Maguires.
    
- A **Pinkerton spy named James McParland infiltrated** the organization and **gathered evidence** (much of it likely fabricated or exaggerated).
    
- The trials relied almost **entirely on Pinkerton testimony**, with **no real due process**.
    

#### **The Executions (1877–1879)**

- **20 Irish miners were hanged** for **alleged murders and acts of sabotage**, many of whom were **likely innocent** or **framed by the Pinkertons**.
    
- **June 21, 1877 ("Black Thursday")**: **10 men were hanged on the same day** in Pennsylvania—**the largest mass execution of civilian labor activists in U.S. history**.
    
- **Other executions followed in 1878 and 1879**.
    

#### **Aftermath**

- The hangings **broke the back of militant labor organizing in the Pennsylvania coal mines**.
    
- The Molly Maguires became **folk legends** in Irish-American and labor history, seen as **martyrs of class struggle**.
    
- **Decades later, some of those executed were posthumously pardoned** after evidence surfaced that the trials were corrupt.
    

---

### **2. Was the Anti-Pinkerton Act Actually Enforced?**

**Short answer: Not really.** The **Anti-Pinkerton Act (1893)** was supposed to prevent **private mercenary forces like the Pinkertons** from working for the federal government, but there were **major loopholes and weak enforcement**.

#### **What the Act Actually Did:**

- Banned **the U.S. government** from **hiring Pinkerton detectives or “similar organizations.”**
    
- The language was **vague**, and corporations were **still free to use them** for union-busting.
    

#### **How It Was Undermined:**

1. **Loopholes & Workarounds**
    
    - The act only **banned hiring Pinkertons directly**—but corporations could still **use them and then coordinate with state forces**.
        
    - Companies **created “new” private security firms** that operated **just like the Pinkertons** under different names.
        
2. **Weak Enforcement**
    
    - The federal government **still used private security forces**, often ignoring the law.
        
    - By the 20th century, similar agencies like **Burns Detective Agency** (later absorbed into the FBI) carried out **the same repressive functions**.
        
3. **The Rise of State-Backed Union Busting**
    
    - By the 1920s, federal and local **law enforcement took over the Pinkerton role**, leading to **massive crackdowns on labor, anarchists, and socialists** (e.g., **Palmer Raids, Red Scare, COINTELPRO**).
        

#### **Long-Term Impact**

- The **Pinkerton name declined**, but its function **was absorbed into corporate-backed law enforcement**.
    
- In the **Reagan era (1980s)**, new **private security forces** emerged that **acted just like the Pinkertons**, working for major corporations to suppress labor and environmental activists.
    

---

### **Final Thought**

- **The Molly Maguires executions** were **a pivotal moment** in **labor repression**, proving how corporations and **private forces like the Pinkertons** could **manipulate the legal system to destroy militant worker resistance**.
    
- The **Anti-Pinkerton Act** was **a symbolic victory but didn’t actually stop private forces from being used against workers**—instead, those functions **were absorbed into the state itself**.
    

This all fits into the broader pattern: **the state and capital adapt their repressive tactics over time, but the underlying dynamic of class war remains the same.** Does that track with how you see things?

Remind me what cointelpro is

###### ChatGPT said:

### **COINTELPRO (Counter Intelligence Program): The U.S. Government’s War on Radical Movements**

COINTELPRO (**Counter Intelligence Program**) was a **secret FBI program** from **1956 to 1971** designed to **disrupt, discredit, and destroy radical political movements** in the U.S., including anarchists, socialists, civil rights organizations, Black liberation movements, and labor organizers.

---

### **1. Goals & Tactics**

The FBI—under **J. Edgar Hoover**—considered **radical leftist movements, Black liberation groups, and anti-war activists a threat to national security** and used **covert, illegal methods** to neutralize them.

#### **Tactics Included:**

- **Surveillance & Wiretapping** – Secretly monitoring activists, even those who weren’t breaking laws.
    
- **Infiltration & Provocateurs** – Placing **spies within organizations** to cause distrust, incite violence, or sabotage organizing efforts.
    
- **Blackmail & Psychological Warfare** – Sending **fake letters** to create internal divisions, threatening activists’ families, or spreading false rumors.
    
- **Arrests & Frame-Ups** – Using **bogus charges** to jail movement leaders.
    
- **Assassination & Police Raids** – Directly or indirectly **aiding in the murders of radical leaders**.
    

---

### **2. Major COINTELPRO Targets**

COINTELPRO wasn’t just aimed at one group—it was a **broad counterinsurgency effort against the left and civil rights movements.**

#### **Black Liberation Movements**

- **The Black Panther Party** – The **#1 target** of COINTELPRO. The FBI infiltrated the Panthers, sowed **internal divisions**, and coordinated with **local police to assassinate leaders** like **Fred Hampton (1969)**.
    
- **Martin Luther King Jr.** – The FBI harassed King for years, **sending him anonymous letters urging him to commit suicide** and surveilling his personal life to discredit him.
    
- **Malcolm X** – COINTELPRO monitored him heavily, and while the FBI didn’t directly assassinate him, **they played a role in sowing division within the Nation of Islam** that contributed to his killing.
    

#### **Leftist & Anti-War Movements**

- **The Weather Underground & SDS (Students for a Democratic Society)** – FBI informants infiltrated anti-war groups to encourage **reckless acts of violence** that could be used as justification for crackdowns.
    
- **The American Indian Movement (AIM)** – COINTELPRO targeted Indigenous activists, leading to the wrongful conviction of **Leonard Peltier** after a deadly shootout at Wounded Knee (1973).
    

#### **Anarchists & Labor Organizers**

- **The Industrial Workers of the World (IWW)** – The FBI continued long-standing government suppression of radical labor, monitoring Wobblies as “subversives.”
    
- **Various Anarchist Groups** – While COINTELPRO focused more on socialists and Black radicals, anarchist groups were also **infiltrated, harassed, and blacklisted.**
    

---

### **3. Exposure & Aftermath**

- COINTELPRO was **exposed in 1971** when activists **broke into an FBI office** in **Media, Pennsylvania**, and leaked files to the press.
    
- The **Church Committee Hearings (1975–76)** revealed COINTELPRO’s **illegal activities**, forcing the FBI to **publicly shut it down**.
    

However, **similar surveillance and repression tactics continued under new names**:

- **The war on "domestic terrorists" post-9/11** revived many COINTELPRO-style tactics against **anti-globalization activists, anarchists, and Muslim communities**.
    
- **The FBI & DHS still monitor Black activists and radical left groups today**, as revealed in leaked documents about **the “Black Identity Extremist” label (2017)**.
    

---

### **Final Thought**

COINTELPRO was **not just about spying—it was about neutralizing radical movements by any means necessary**, including **murder, false charges, and psychological warfare**. While officially dismantled, **the playbook of state repression against leftist movements never really disappeared**—it simply **evolved**.

Would you like to go deeper into **modern versions of COINTELPRO**, or are you more interested in specific case studies (like Fred Hampton’s assassination)?

##### You said:

Are you aware of what cafiero said to the peasants of Letino?

###### ChatGPT said:

Yes! **Carlo Cafiero**, an Italian anarchist and close collaborator of **Bakunin**, played a key role in early anarchist uprisings in Italy. His most famous moment came in **1877**, during an **attempted insurrection in Letino**, a small village in southern Italy.

---

### **The Letino Uprising (1877) & Cafiero’s Speech to the Peasants**

In **April 1877**, Cafiero, **Errico Malatesta**, and a small group of anarchists **took over the town of Letino** in an attempt to spark a peasant revolution. They **burned tax records, freed prisoners, and distributed goods**, believing this direct action would inspire mass revolt.

While in Letino, **Cafiero addressed the peasants**, saying something to the effect of:

> **“The revolution does not bring liberty; it only clears the way for liberty to be taken by those who wish for it.”**

This statement **captures the anarchist idea that liberation is not something granted by leaders or revolutionary vanguards—but something people must seize for themselves**.

- Cafiero and his comrades believed that **anarchism could not be imposed from above**, even by revolutionaries.
    
- They saw their role as **helping people break free from oppression**, but not **governing them or replacing one authority with another**.
    
- This is in stark contrast to **Marxist-led revolutions**, which often sought to **seize state power first, then implement socialism “from above.”**
    

---

### **What Happened Next?**

- **Initial Support, but No Widespread Uprising** – The peasants of Letino **welcomed the anarchists** at first, but **did not rise up en masse**, likely due to fear of retaliation.
    
- **Defeat & Arrest** – The insurrection **quickly collapsed** when Italian troops arrived. Cafiero, Malatesta, and others were **captured and imprisoned**.
    
- **Long-Term Impact** – Even though the uprising failed, it became **a key moment in Italian anarchist history**, demonstrating both **the potential and limits of spontaneous revolutionary action**.
    

---

### **Final Thought:**

Cafiero’s words reflect **a deeply anarchist approach to revolution**—one that **rejects top-down control and insists that freedom must be actively taken by the people themselves**.

Do you think this kind of **bottom-up revolutionary attempt** could work in modern times, or is it a strategy that only made sense in that era?

##### You said:

Tell me more about the Russian nihilist movement that preceded the anarchists

###### ChatGPT said:

The **Russian Nihilist movement** of the mid-to-late 19th century was a radical intellectual and political movement that **rejected all traditional authorities, institutions, and moral conventions** in pursuit of a new, rational and egalitarian society. It **preceded and influenced Russian anarchism**, shaping the revolutionary climate that later figures like **Bakunin and Kropotkin** would emerge from.

---

### **1. What Did Russian Nihilists Believe?**

The **nihilists** were not merely pessimists or philosophers of meaninglessness (as the modern term might suggest)—they were **political radicals** who **rejected Tsarist autocracy, religious dogma, aristocratic privilege, and cultural conservatism**.

- **Radical Skepticism** – They questioned all **authority, moral values, and inherited traditions**, seeing them as tools of oppression.
    
- **Science & Rationalism** – Nihilists **rejected religious belief** and **favored scientific materialism**, often embracing atheism.
    
- **Personal Liberation** – Many sought **total individual freedom**, including **sexual liberation, gender equality, and the rejection of marriage** (which they saw as a patriarchal institution).
    
- **Revolutionary Direct Action** – By the 1860s, many nihilists **moved toward political violence**, believing that **terrorism and assassination could destabilize the regime**.
    

The **nihilist slogan**, popularized by **Dmitry Pisarev**, was:

> **“The most useful person is the one who destroys the most obstacles to progress.”**

This **direct-action mentality** would later be adopted by **anarchists, socialist revolutionaries, and even early Bolsheviks**.

---

### **2. Key Figures & Groups**

#### **Nikolai Chernyshevsky (1828–1889)** – The Intellectual Catalyst

- His novel **"What Is to Be Done?" (1863)** became **a foundational text** for the nihilist movement.
    
- It portrayed **a radical vanguard** working to destroy old institutions and build a new society.
    
- **Lenin later took the book’s title for his own revolutionary pamphlet (1902).**
    

#### **Sergey Nechaev (1847–1882)** – The Revolutionary Conspirator

- Author of **"The Revolutionary Catechism" (1869)**, a manual for **ruthless revolutionary discipline**.
    
- He argued that **a revolutionary must be completely detached from personal relationships, morality, and compassion**—devoted only to the cause.
    
- Nechaev formed **a secret cell that engaged in political murder**, including the infamous **assassination of one of their own members for suspected betrayal**.
    
- **Bakunin initially admired Nechaev**, but later **denounced his authoritarianism and paranoia**.
    

#### **"Land and Liberty" (Zemlya i Volya, 1860s-70s)** – From Nihilism to Revolutionary Populism

- One of the first **underground Russian revolutionary organizations**.
    
- Advocated for **going to the peasants** to spread revolutionary ideas—a strategy later used by anarchists and Narodniks.
    
- Eventually split into **terrorist factions** that carried out assassinations of Tsarist officials.
    

---

### **3. The Shift Toward Anarchism & Socialism**

By the **1870s and 1880s**, Russian nihilism evolved into more structured **anarchist and socialist movements**:

- **From Nihilism to Anarchism**
    
    - Figures like **Bakunin and Kropotkin** drew from nihilist rejection of authority but developed more **cohesive social theories**.
        
    - Nihilism’s emphasis on **destruction** was **merged with anarchist ideas about self-organization, mutual aid, and federated governance**.
        
- **From Nihilism to Narodniks & Bolsheviks**
    
    - Some nihilists moved into **Narodnaya Volya ("The People's Will")**, which focused on **assassinating Tsarist officials** (including Tsar Alexander II in 1881).
        
    - Others evolved into **Marxist factions**, ultimately influencing Lenin and the Bolsheviks, who **adopted their revolutionary discipline and methods** while rejecting anarchism.
        

---

### **4. The Nihilist Legacy**

- **Terrorism as Political Strategy** – They pioneered **the idea of targeted assassinations** as a revolutionary method.
    
- **Radical Atheism & Materialism** – Nihilists helped push Russian intellectual culture **away from religious mysticism** and toward **scientific socialism and anarchism**.
    
- **Influence on Lenin & Bolshevism** – Lenin borrowed **both the organizational secrecy and the brutal discipline** of figures like Nechaev and groups like **The People's Will**.
    
- **Cultural Impact** – Nihilists set the stage for **the Russian avant-garde, futurism, and radical philosophy**, influencing figures from **Dostoevsky (who wrote against them in _Demons_) to the revolutionary artists of the early USSR**.
    

---

### **Final Thought**

Russian nihilism **wasn’t a fully formed political movement like anarchism or socialism**, but it **created the conditions for radical revolution** by **destroying faith in traditional power structures**. It was the **match that lit the fire**—but anarchists, socialists, and Bolsheviks would **debate how best to wield the flames**.

##### You said:

Helpful info — tell me more about that quote from pisarev, how would they have defined “progress” here

###### ChatGPT said:

Great question—**Dmitry Pisarev** and the **nihilists had a very specific and radical definition of "progress."** When he wrote:

> **“The most useful person is the one who destroys the most obstacles to progress.”**

he wasn’t talking about **gradual reform or measured societal improvement**—he meant **total destruction of outdated, oppressive institutions and ideas** in order to **clear space for a new, rational, egalitarian society.**

### **How Nihilists Defined “Progress”**

Unlike liberal reformers or even some socialists of the time, nihilists saw **progress as an uncompromising process of elimination**—not a step-by-step improvement within the existing system, but the **removal of everything irrational, superstitious, and oppressive.**

#### **1. The Destruction of Old Institutions**

For Pisarev and the nihilists, **progress first meant the absolute rejection of traditional power structures**:

- **The Tsarist Autocracy** – They saw the Russian monarchy as an inherently **violent, backward system that needed to be completely overthrown.**
    
- **The Orthodox Church** – Religion was viewed as **a tool of control, irrational mysticism, and submission to authority.**
    
- **The Aristocracy & Bourgeoisie** – Wealth and privilege based on **birth, landownership, or status** had to be dismantled.
    

#### **2. The Abolition of Cultural and Social Norms**

Nihilists weren’t just political radicals—they wanted to **abolish all inherited traditions that they saw as reinforcing oppression.**

- **Marriage & Family Structures** – They believed the nuclear family was a **patriarchal institution that reinforced obedience and property inheritance**.
    
- **Gender Roles** – Nihilist women **rejected corsets, refused arranged marriages, and demanded access to education and professions.**
    
- **Aesthetic & Literary Tradition** – They saw **Romantic literature and art as decadent distractions** and favored **scientific realism**.
    

#### **3. Rationalism, Science, and Materialism**

Nihilists were deeply influenced by **scientific materialism and utilitarianism**—they saw **science and reason as the only legitimate paths forward**.

- **Education & Science as the New Foundation** – Schools should teach **pure rationalism, not religious or nationalistic propaganda.**
    
- **Anti-Idealism** – Philosophies like Hegelianism and metaphysical speculation were dismissed as **useless abstractions**.
    
- **Utilitarianism** – Every action should be judged by its **material benefit to society, not abstract morality.**
    

#### **4. The Justification for Destruction**

Pisarev’s quote implies that **breaking down barriers is itself a form of progress**—even if the people doing the destruction don’t immediately build something new.

- This idea **mirrors Bakunin’s later phrase**:
    
    > **“The passion for destruction is a creative passion, too.”**
    
- Nihilists saw destruction as **a means to an end, not an end in itself**—once the obstacles were gone, **a rational, equal society could be built.**
    

---

### **How This Compared to Other Revolutionary Thinkers**

- **Liberal Reformers** wanted **gradual changes to Russian law & institutions** while keeping a monarchy.
    
- **Marxists** wanted a **scientific, structured approach** to dismantling capitalism and replacing it with **a state-run transition to socialism.**
    
- **Anarchists** like Bakunin agreed with nihilists on **the destruction of state and church** but emphasized **self-organization and voluntary communities.**
    
- **Nihilists** believed in **pure destruction as the starting point, trusting that a new society would emerge from the rubble.**
    

---

### **Final Thought**

Pisarev’s definition of **progress as destruction** reflected the nihilist belief that **old systems could not be reformed or salvaged—they had to be completely wiped out.** This was a **precursor to more organized revolutionary movements**, but it also exposed a major weakness: **nihilists were better at tearing things down than building new structures to replace them.**

That’s why later movements, like anarchism and socialism, had to **grapple with what comes after destruction.**

##### You said:

What would have been the lowest economic rung of the bourgeoisie during the late 1800s? Just trying to understand how those in today’s “upper middle class” may have been viewed. I know it’s not apples to apples, but any context would be helpful

###### ChatGPT said:

That’s a great question. The **late 19th-century bourgeoisie** was not a monolithic class—it had **tiers**, ranging from ultra-wealthy industrialists to small business owners who were **not far removed from the working class**. The **lowest economic rung of the bourgeoisie** in this period would have included **petite bourgeoisie (small business owners, self-employed professionals, and skilled tradespeople)**—the historical equivalent of today’s **upper middle class.**

---

### **Understanding the Late 1800s Bourgeoisie Strata**

#### **1. The _Haute Bourgeoisie_ (High Bourgeoisie) – The Capitalist Elite**

- **Industrialists, bankers, large factory owners, and railroad tycoons.**
    
- Often had **political influence** and were **major benefactors of state policies** (e.g., monopolies, protective tariffs).
    
- Would be comparable to today’s **billionaires and major corporate CEOs.**
    

#### **2. The _Moyenne Bourgeoisie_ (Middle Bourgeoisie) – The Managerial Class**

- **Upper-level managers, medium-sized business owners, successful merchants, and professionals (doctors, lawyers, high-ranking civil servants).**
    
- Often **owned capital but didn’t control entire industries.**
    
- Had a **comfortable but not ultra-wealthy** lifestyle.
    
- Similar to today’s **corporate executives, partners in law firms, and high-earning professionals**.
    

#### **3. The _Petite Bourgeoisie_ (Lower Bourgeoisie) – Small Proprietors & Skilled Professionals**

- **Shopkeepers, artisans, independent tradespeople, teachers, low-level public officials, and small-scale factory owners.**
    
- **Did not own large capital investments**—often self-employed or running businesses with **very small margins**.
    
- **Economically unstable**—many faced **proletarianization** (the process of being pushed into the working class).
    
- Would be similar to **today’s upper middle class: small business owners, well-paid professionals, and mid-level managers.**
    

---

### **How Would Today’s "Upper Middle Class" Have Been Viewed?**

If we think of today’s **upper middle class (small business owners, tech workers, doctors, professors, corporate professionals, etc.)**, they would probably **map onto the petite bourgeoisie of the 19th century.**

**How were they viewed by radicals like anarchists and Marxists?**

✅ **Seen as politically unreliable** – Often caught between siding with the working class or aspiring to be part of the wealthier bourgeoisie.

✅ **Seen as potential allies (but often in a precarious position)** – Anarchists and Marxists **sometimes saw them as victims of capitalism** since many small business owners were eventually crushed by industrial monopolies.

✅ **Criticized for self-interest** – They often prioritized **their own property and businesses over broader class solidarity**, making them **hesitant revolutionaries**.

✅ **Some became revolutionaries (but often from crisis, not ideology)** – When squeezed by economic downturns, they **could radicalize**, as seen in the **Paris Commune (1871)** and later in **fascist movements (which also recruited from this class).**

---

### **Final Thought: Then vs. Now**

- **Similarities:** Both the **19th-century petite bourgeoisie and today’s upper middle class** are caught in a **precarious position between capital and labor**, with many holding **reactionary, self-preserving views** unless economic collapse radicalizes them.
    
- **Differences:** Today’s **upper middle class benefits from modern finance, tech, and corporate structures**, whereas 19th-century petite bourgeoisie were often **independent business owners subject to boom-and-bust cycles**.
    

It’s not an exact 1:1 comparison, but understanding this historical parallel helps explain **why today’s upper middle class tends to resist revolutionary movements while still being vulnerable to economic instability.**

##### You said:

Tell me more about the petite bourgeoise and the Paris commune

###### ChatGPT said:

### **The Petite Bourgeoisie & The Paris Commune (1871)**

The **petite bourgeoisie** (lower middle class) played a **complicated, sometimes contradictory role** in the **Paris Commune of 1871**. While many of them were actively involved in the uprising, others **hesitated** or even opposed it, showing the class’s tendency to be **caught between revolution and self-preservation**.

---

### **1. Who Were the Petite Bourgeoisie in 1871?**

By the late 19th century, the **petite bourgeoisie** in Paris included:  
✅ **Small shopkeepers, artisans, and self-employed craftsmen**  
✅ **Schoolteachers, clerks, and lower-level public employees**  
✅ **Small business owners and independent professionals**

They were **economically distinct from the working class (proletariat)** but also **didn’t own major capital like the haute bourgeoisie** (industrialists and bankers). Many were **highly educated but economically precarious**, making them **hesitant revolutionaries** (hence your keynotes entry!).

---

### **2. Their Role in the Paris Commune**

During the **Franco-Prussian War (1870-71)**, economic hardship radicalized much of the **petite bourgeoisie**, leading them to **align with the working class** in revolutionary action.

#### **A. Early Support for the Commune**

- Many **small shopkeepers and artisans were strongly anti-monarchist and anti-imperialist**—they supported the Commune’s democratic ideals.
    
- **Schoolteachers and lower-level civil servants joined in**, seeing the Commune as a **chance to create an egalitarian, decentralized society**.
    
- **Proudhonian mutualists (influenced by early anarchism)** in the petite bourgeoisie supported the Commune’s emphasis on **workers’ cooperatives and self-management**.
    

#### **B. Divisions & Hesitations**

Despite their early enthusiasm, the **petite bourgeoisie was divided** once the Commune took power:

✅ **Some embraced radical measures** – They supported policies like **worker-run factories and social programs**, aligning with anarchists and revolutionary socialists.

❌ **Some feared expropriation** – Shopkeepers and small business owners worried about **losing their property**, making them **reluctant to support full-scale socialist policies**.

❌ **Some turned against the Commune** – The wealthier members of the petite bourgeoisie (who employed workers) **felt threatened by growing worker control** and sided with counter-revolutionaries.

---

### **3. The Fall of the Commune & the Petite Bourgeoisie’s Fate**

After **two months of self-governance**, the **French national government crushed the Paris Commune in May 1871** during **"La Semaine Sanglante" (The Bloody Week).**

- The **working-class revolutionaries suffered the most**, with **20,000+ executed or exiled**.
    
- The petite bourgeoisie had a **mixed fate**:
    
    - Some **escaped repression** by proving they had not been full Communards.
        
    - Others were still **executed or imprisoned**, especially teachers and civil servants who had aligned with the Commune.
        
    - Some **abandoned radicalism entirely**, becoming more conservative in the years after.
        

---

### **4. Legacy: Why This Still Matters**

- The **petite bourgeoisie in the Commune revealed the class contradictions that still exist today**—torn between **alliances with workers and fears of losing status/property**.
    
- **Marx later argued that the petite bourgeoisie would always be unreliable revolutionaries**—too dependent on capitalism to fully commit to socialism, but too oppressed to be fully capitalist.
    
- **Anarchists saw the Commune as proof that self-governance was possible** but also that class collaboration had limits—**true revolution had to be bottom-up and not dependent on hesitant allies**.
    

---

### **Final Thought**

The **petite bourgeoisie’s role in the Commune reflects its historical pattern**—sometimes radical, sometimes reactionary, but always **stuck between revolution and self-interest**. This dynamic still plays out today with **small business owners, professionals, and the educated middle class** who often **sympathize with worker struggles but hesitate when real systemic change is on the table**.

###### ChatGPT said:

The **Confederación Nacional del Trabajo (CNT)**, established in early 20th-century Spain, is renowned for its **anarcho-syndicalist principles**, emphasizing **direct action, self-management, and horizontal organizational structures**. These principles have influenced various social movements, including the **Occupy movement** that began in 2011.

The **Occupy movement** was characterized by its **leaderless, horizontal "structure"**, and the strategy of setting up camps in public areas for extended periods. [researchgate.net](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/235624196_The_%27Occupy%27_Movement_Emerging_Protest_Forms_and_Contested_Urban_Spaces?utm_source=chatgpt.com) This approach reflects a commitment to **anarchist ideals**, even if not explicitly labeled as such. [theanarchistlibrary.org](https://theanarchistlibrary.org/library/morgan-rodgers-gibson-the-anarchism-of-the-occupy-movement?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

While the Occupy movement did not directly cite the CNT as a model, its organizational ethos aligns with the **anarcho-syndicalist tradition** that the CNT embodies. This suggests that the movement drew inspiration from a broader historical context of anarchist federations and their governance models.

In summary, the Occupy movement's governance structures resonate with the principles championed by the CNT and similar anarchist organizations, indicating an indirect influence through shared ideals of decentralized, participatory organization.

##### You said:

What do you know about the structure of the CNT ?

###### ChatGPT said:

The **Confederación Nacional del Trabajo (CNT)** is a **Spanish anarcho-syndicalist labor federation** that has operated since **1910** and was particularly influential during the **Spanish Civil War (1936–1939)**. Unlike traditional labor unions that negotiate within capitalist frameworks, the CNT sought to **abolish capitalism and the state** through **direct action, worker self-management, and decentralized federations**.

---

### **1. CNT’s Organizational Structure: Federalist & Bottom-Up**

The CNT **rejected hierarchy**, operating as a **confederal structure**, meaning **all decision-making flowed from the workers and local assemblies upward, rather than from leaders downward**.

#### **A. The Basic Unit: The Sindicato Único (Local Union)**

- The **core organizational unit** was the **Syndicate (Sindicato Único)**, composed of **workers in the same industry or workplace.**
    
- Each sindicato operated **autonomously**, deciding its own policies **through direct democracy in assemblies**.
    

#### **B. Local Federations (Comarcal and Regional)**

- **Sindicatos federated at the local (comarcal) and regional levels** to coordinate broader struggles.
    
- No **centralized leadership**—each federation was **independent but coordinated actions voluntarily**.
    

#### **C. The National Confederation (Confederación)**

- **A loose, voluntary confederation of all regional unions**, used for coordination, not control.
    
- **No executive authority**—power remained at the local level.
    
- Any **national decision had to be approved by local unions** before implementation.
    

#### **D. Committees for Coordination, Not Leadership**

- **Administrative committees existed, but they had no coercive power**.
    
- The **Permanent Secretariat** helped coordinate national activity but could not make unilateral decisions.
    

---

### **2. Key Principles of CNT Organization**

✅ **Direct Democracy** – Decisions were made in **open assemblies**, ensuring **every worker had a say**.  
✅ **Mandate & Recall** – Delegates were **strictly mandated** by their assemblies and could be **recalled at any time**.  
✅ **No Paid Bureaucracy** – The CNT rejected **union bosses** and **professional leadership**—all representatives were **rank-and-file workers**.  
✅ **Autonomy & Decentralization** – **Each local union had full autonomy**, coordinating voluntarily with others.  
✅ **Direct Action Over Negotiation** – The CNT **prioritized strikes, occupations, and worker takeovers over legalistic bargaining**.

---

### **3. Influence on the Spanish Revolution (1936-1939)**

- The CNT played a **crucial role in collectivizing workplaces, farms, and entire industries** during the Spanish Civil War.
    
- **Workers took over factories, abolished money in some areas, and ran society collectively without bosses.**
    
- **The coordination of large-scale industry (e.g., Barcelona’s public transit system) showed that decentralized self-management could work at scale.**
    

---

### **4. Legacy & Influence on Modern Movements**

The CNT’s structure influenced many later anti-authoritarian labor movements, including:

- **The Industrial Workers of the World (IWW)** – Shared the commitment to **direct action, worker self-management, and no paid bureaucracy**.
    
- **Occupy Movement’s General Assemblies** – Though not directly modeled on the CNT, Occupy’s emphasis on **horizontal, consensus-driven decision-making** reflects anarcho-syndicalist principles.
    
- **Contemporary Autonomous Worker Cooperatives** – Many modern worker co-ops operate with **non-hierarchical, CNT-inspired decision-making structures**.
    

---

### **Final Thought**

The CNT’s **federated, non-hierarchical structure** made it one of history’s most **successful experiments in anarchist organization**. It proved that **large-scale worker coordination without bosses, bureaucrats, or political elites was possible**—but it also faced challenges from **external repression and internal strategic divisions**.

Would you like a breakdown of **how the CNT managed large-scale worker self-management** during the Spanish Revolution?

##### You said:

Yeah!

###### ChatGPT said:

### **How the CNT Managed Large-Scale Worker Self-Management (1936–1939)**

During the **Spanish Revolution (1936-1939)**, the **CNT** and its sister anarcho-syndicalist organization **the Federación Anarquista Ibérica (FAI)** played a key role in **organizing worker self-management at an unprecedented scale**. When the Spanish Civil War broke out, instead of waiting for political leaders to act, **workers took over factories, farms, and public services**—running them without bosses, capitalists, or state intervention.

---

## **1. How Workplaces & Industries Were Taken Over**

✅ **Direct Worker Expropriation**

- As the Spanish government collapsed in **July 1936**, CNT-led workers **seized workplaces**, including **factories, farms, railways, hospitals, and public utilities**.
    
- Business owners, managers, and landlords fled or were dismissed, and **control was transferred to workers’ committees**.
    

✅ **General Assemblies as Decision-Making Bodies**

- Each workplace or collective was run by **general assemblies** where **all workers participated in decision-making**.
    
- **Mandated, recallable delegates** were chosen for **coordination, not authority**.
    

✅ **Federated Industry Coordination**

- Individual workplaces **federated together**, coordinating at the city and regional levels.
    
- Entire **industries (like transportation, health care, and agriculture) were run collectively**, ensuring efficient supply chains.
    

---

## **2. Large-Scale Industry Management**

One of the most remarkable aspects of the CNT’s self-management experiment was that it **wasn’t limited to small cooperatives—entire industries were collectivized and run efficiently**.

### **A. Urban Industries: Barcelona’s Worker-Controlled Economy**

Barcelona, the CNT’s stronghold, saw **over 70% of industry collectivized** within weeks.

- **Public Transport:** CNT workers took over **Barcelona’s entire bus and tram system**, ensuring daily operations **without bosses**.
    
- **Factories:** CNT-led worker committees **continued production in key industries**, including weapons manufacturing to fight the fascists.
    
- **Health Care:** **CNT-led medical syndicates took over hospitals**, introducing **free, worker-run medical care** for the first time in Spanish history.
    

### **B. Rural Collectives: Agrarian Anarchism in Aragon & Catalonia**

- In the countryside, **peasants expropriated landowners’ estates and formed self-managed communes**.
    
- **Money was abolished in many areas**, replaced by **a system of free exchange and rationing**.
    
- **Collectives produced surplus food and distributed it to urban areas**, ensuring supply chains functioned.
    

---

## **3. Economic Coordination Without a State**

One of the most radical aspects of the CNT’s model was that **they managed large-scale production and distribution without a state or capitalist system**.

✅ **The CNT created industrial federations to organize production & logistics.**  
✅ **Neighborhood committees ensured rationing and fair distribution of goods.**  
✅ **Worker-run factories coordinated directly with each other**, rather than relying on state planning.  
✅ **Railroads, telegraphs, and shipping were collectivized**, keeping communication and trade running.

Instead of **centralized economic planning**, the CNT used a **federalist system of self-managed workplaces, coordinated from the bottom up**.

---

## **4. Challenges & Internal Struggles**

Despite its successes, worker self-management faced **major challenges**:

### **A. Military & Fascist Pressure**

- The CNT had to **balance workplace management with fighting Franco’s fascist forces** in the Spanish Civil War.
    
- **Factory workers had to maintain production while also organizing militias to defend the revolution.**
    

### **B. Conflict with Soviet-Backed Communists**

- The CNT’s **stateless, bottom-up model** clashed with the Soviet-backed **Spanish Communist Party (PCE)**, which sought **state-controlled socialism**.
    
- In **May 1937, Communist forces violently crushed CNT-led collectivized industries in Barcelona**, returning control to the government.
    

### **C. Lack of Material Resources**

- Due to economic blockades and war conditions, CNT collectives **struggled with shortages of raw materials, fuel, and machinery**.
    
- **International trade was difficult** because foreign governments **refused to recognize or trade with anarchist-run collectives**.
    

---

## **5. The Fall of the Revolution (1939)**

By 1939, **Franco’s fascist forces defeated the Republican side**, leading to the destruction of the **CNT’s collectivized economy**.

- Thousands of anarchist militants and workers **were executed or exiled**.
    
- **Collectivized industries and land were seized** and returned to capitalists and landowners.
    
- The Spanish government **outlawed the CNT**, forcing it underground.
    

However, the **CNT’s experiment in large-scale worker self-management remains one of the most significant anarchist achievements in history**—proving that **industry, agriculture, and public services can be run without bosses, state bureaucracy, or capitalist profit motives**.

---

## **Final Thought**

The CNT’s revolution **demonstrated the viability of large-scale anarchist organization** but also **exposed the vulnerabilities of stateless revolutions when facing military opposition from both fascists and authoritarian socialists**.

Would you like a breakdown of **specific anarchist-run industries** during the revolution, or a comparison with other historical worker self-management experiments?

##### You said:

It’s just surprising not to hear CNT cited as a model for radical collectives of today. Unless I’m unaware of movements who call them out specifically?

###### ChatGPT said:

It's understandable to find it surprising that the **Confederación Nacional del Trabajo (CNT)** isn't more frequently cited as a model by contemporary radical collectives. While the CNT's historical significance in promoting anarcho-syndicalism and worker self-management during the Spanish Civil War is well-documented, its direct influence on modern movements appears to be less pronounced.

However, the CNT's legacy continues through organizations like the **General Confederation of Labour (CGT)** in Spain, which emerged from a split with the CNT during Spain's transition to democracy. The CGT has become one of the largest trade unions in Spain, advocating for self-management and social ownership, particularly in industries like automotive, telecommunications, and transportation. Their activities reflect the enduring principles of the CNT, even if the original organization isn't explicitly referenced. [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Confederation_of_Labour_%28Spain%29?utm_source=chatgpt.com)

In summary, while the CNT may not be frequently cited by name, its foundational principles and organizational models have permeated various contemporary movements and organizations, influencing their structures and strategies in advocating for worker self-management and direct action.

##### You said:

How would bakunin have viewed modern American trade unions versus those he was familiar with in his time? What critiques would he have had?

###### ChatGPT said:

Bakunin’s view of **modern American trade unions** would likely be **deeply critical**, though he would recognize their **potential as revolutionary forces** if they could break free from state and corporate influence. In his time, **trade unions were more decentralized, militant, and often aligned with revolutionary anarchism**—something he saw as crucial for dismantling capitalism and the state. However, today’s large-scale unions are often **bureaucratized, legally constrained, and integrated into state-capitalist structures**, which would go against Bakunin’s vision of working-class self-liberation.

---

### **1. What Were Trade Unions Like in Bakunin’s Time?**

During the mid-to-late **19th century**, when Bakunin was most active, **trade unions were often small, decentralized, and illegal in many countries**. They were:

✅ **Directly tied to revolutionary politics** – Many were anarchist or socialist in orientation.  
✅ **Militant and autonomous** – They relied on **wildcat strikes, sabotage, and mutual aid** rather than legal negotiations.  
✅ **Organized outside the state** – Unions had to operate **in secrecy** or face state repression.

Bakunin saw **early trade unions** as **the embryos of a future stateless society**—small, self-organized groups that could **replace capitalist production with worker-run industry**.

---

### **2. What Would Bakunin Say About Modern American Trade Unions?**

Today’s American trade unions are vastly different from the worker organizations Bakunin admired. He would likely **criticize them for their integration into the state and capitalist system**.

#### **A. Bureaucratization & Union Leadership**

❌ **Unions have hierarchical leadership structures**

- In Bakunin’s time, unions were often **self-managed and horizontal**.
    
- Today, large unions **have entrenched leadership**, with presidents and executives earning high salaries while making decisions on behalf of workers.
    

❌ **Union leaders negotiate rather than agitate**

- Many modern unions rely on **lobbying politicians and legal arbitration** instead of direct action.
    
- Bakunin believed that the **working class should not negotiate with the capitalist state, but overthrow it**.
    

---

#### **B. Integration into the State & Capitalist System**

❌ **Unions are legally bound by capitalist rules**

- Laws like the **Wagner Act (1935)** and **Taft-Hartley Act (1947)** **forced unions to work within legal frameworks** that protect capitalism.
    
- **No-strike clauses, mandatory arbitration, and union contracts** limit their ability to act **outside of state control**.
    

❌ **Unionism as a means of worker management rather than liberation**

- Many unions today act as **mediators between workers and bosses** rather than **forces for revolutionary change**.
    
- Bakunin would see this as a way to **contain worker uprisings rather than empower them**.
    

---

#### **C. Dependence on Electoral Politics**

❌ **Modern unions back political candidates instead of organizing worker power**

- American unions **spend millions on elections** rather than **building independent worker power**.
    
- Bakunin rejected electoral politics, believing **workers should not rely on politicians or political parties for liberation**.
    

✅ **Bakunin would advocate for radical worker autonomy**

- He would support **unions organizing outside the Democratic Party and rejecting corporate funding**.
    
- He would likely push for **wildcat strikes, direct action, and decentralized syndicalism** rather than electoral campaigns.
    

---

### **3. What Would Bakunin’s Alternative Be?**

Bakunin saw unions as a **foundation for a post-capitalist society**—but only if they remained **self-managed, anti-state, and directly revolutionary**. He would likely advocate for:

✅ **Worker-managed syndicates instead of bureaucratic unions** – Similar to the **CNT model in Spain**, where **unions are run by workers directly, not by professional leadership.**  
✅ **Permanent struggle instead of legal contracts** – Instead of signing long-term contracts that limit worker power, he would encourage **constant agitation, strikes, and direct action**.  
✅ **Direct worker control of production** – Instead of **negotiating for better wages within capitalism**, unions should **seize and run workplaces collectively**.

---

### **4. Are There Any Modern Unions Bakunin Would Approve Of?**

Though most large unions wouldn’t meet his standards, he might find some promise in:

✅ **The Industrial Workers of the World (IWW)** – A decentralized, syndicalist union that rejects hierarchy and still promotes direct action.  
✅ **Rank-and-file movements within mainstream unions** – Grassroots labor movements that challenge union bureaucracy and push for **more militant action**.  
✅ **Worker co-ops and autonomous workplace takeovers** – Any efforts where workers **directly control production without bosses or bureaucratic union intermediaries**.

---

### **Final Thought**

Bakunin saw **trade unions as a vehicle for revolutionary change**, but only **if they remained decentralized, directly democratic, and committed to overthrowing capitalism**. Today’s **bureaucratic, state-integrated unions would likely be a disappointment to him**, though he would probably support **rank-and-file insurgencies and syndicalist organizing** as steps toward worker self-liberation.

##### You said:

What were his thoughts on socialism or anarchism?

###### ChatGPT said:

Herbert Marcuse had a **complex and evolving relationship** with both **socialism** and **anarchism**, and while he remained rooted in the **Marxist tradition**, he was **sympathetic to anarchist critiques** of authority and bureaucracy—especially in his later work. Here’s a breakdown:

---

### **1. Socialism: Marcuse’s Marxist Core**

Marcuse was a **critical Marxist**, part of the **Frankfurt School**, and deeply influenced by **Hegelian dialectics and Freudian psychoanalysis**. He believed in **overcoming capitalism**, but he was **critical of the actually existing socialisms** of his time, particularly the **Soviet model**.

#### **What he supported:**

- A **liberated, non-repressive society** built on **Marxist humanism**.
    
- Socialism that **frees human potential**, not just redistributes goods.
    
- The idea that **technology and productivity**, if freed from capitalist imperatives, could **eliminate scarcity** and **enable true freedom**.
    

#### **What he opposed:**

- **Authoritarian socialism**, like the Soviet Union, which he saw as **bureaucratic and repressive**, not liberatory.
    
- The idea that **progressive material conditions alone** would lead to human emancipation—he thought **subjectivity and imagination** were essential too.
    

---

### **2. Anarchism: Philosophically Aligned, Politically Distant**

Marcuse **never called himself an anarchist**, but he **admired the spirit of anarchism**—especially its **anti-authoritarianism, utopian imagination, and emphasis on spontaneity**.

- He praised **Bakunin’s** critique of Marxism’s authoritarian tendencies.
    
- He expressed solidarity with **student uprisings** and **New Left anarchist movements** in the 1960s, which rejected both capitalism and Soviet-style socialism.
    
- He saw **revolutionary subjectivity**, especially among **marginalized groups (students, Black radicals, the Third World)**, as vital—this **echoes anarchist emphasis on will and direct action**.
    

#### From _One-Dimensional Man_:

- He critiques not only capitalist society but **how even opposition is absorbed and neutralized** (what he calls **“repressive tolerance”**).
    
- He’s skeptical of mass political parties and union bureaucracies—common anarchist concerns.
    
- He pushes for **new forms of consciousness and imagination**—something anarchists also emphasize.
    

---

### **3. His Utopian Leanings**

Marcuse believed that **true liberation required a total break from one-dimensional thinking**, which includes not just the economy but **culture, aesthetics, and desire**. This aligns closely with **anarchist ideas of prefigurative politics**—living the new society in the shell of the old.

---

### **In Summary:**

- **Socialist in theory**, but **anti-authoritarian in practice**.
    
- **Not an anarchist**, but **deeply aligned with anarchism’s cultural and philosophical spirit**.
    
- Saw **hope in radical subjectivity**, imagination, and new forms of resistance, much like **anarchist emphasis on spontaneity, autonomy, and refusal**.
    

If you're re-reading _One-Dimensional Man_, you're likely to see **how close his critique runs to a lot of anarchist concerns**, even if he stayed in the Marxist camp. Let me know if you want to track those parallels as you read!

##### You said:

What does it mean when he says technological rationality has become political rationality?

###### ChatGPT said:

When Marcuse says **technological rationality has become political rationality**, he means that the logic once used to **organize and optimize machines, production, and systems**—what we’d call **technical efficiency**—has now **become the dominant logic of social and political life**. In other words, **efficiency, control, and functionality** have **replaced values like freedom, justice, or critical thinking** as the **primary measures of what is “rational” or “good.”**

---

### **Breaking It Down:**

#### **1. Technological Rationality:**

- Refers to a mindset that values:
    
    - **Efficiency**
        
    - **Optimization**
        
    - **Productivity**
        
    - **Control**
        
- It’s the kind of logic that governs machines, workflows, and engineering.
    

#### **2. Political Rationality:**

- Refers to how decisions are made in society—what’s considered **reasonable**, **necessary**, or **acceptable** in governance, law, economics, etc.
    

---

### **Marcuse’s Claim:**

In advanced industrial society, the **technological mindset has infiltrated politics and culture** so deeply that:

- **Social questions are framed as technical problems** (“How do we manage poverty?” not “Why does poverty exist?”)
    
- **Freedom is defined as increased consumer choice** or access to efficient systems—not emancipation.
    
- **Dissent is irrationalized** if it doesn’t fit into this framework (“That’s not feasible,” “That’s inefficient,” “That’s utopian”).
    

---

### **The Consequences:**

- **Critical thinking is stifled**—because all alternative visions are dismissed as irrational or impractical.
    
- **One-dimensional thought dominates**—people stop imagining truly different futures, because the current system appears “natural” or “inevitable.”
    
- **Technological progress masks domination**—new tools or comforts distract from the fact that human freedom is still limited.
    

Marcuse isn’t anti-technology—but he’s warning that when **technical logic governs politics**, it **naturalizes existing power relations**, **erases radical alternatives**, and **turns people into passive consumers rather than active agents of change.**

---

Let me know if you'd like some contemporary examples of this idea—it pops up everywhere from urban planning to workplace surveillance.

##### You said:

Can you summarize chapter two, "The Closing of the political universe"

###### ChatGPT said:

Absolutely. Chapter Two of _One-Dimensional Man_, titled **"The Closing of the Political Universe,"** explores how **advanced industrial society absorbs and neutralizes political opposition**, making real alternatives to the status quo almost unthinkable. Here’s a summary of the key ideas:

---

### **Chapter Two Summary: "The Closing of the Political Universe"**

Marcuse argues that in modern industrial societies—both capitalist and communist—the **realm of politics has been collapsed into the logic of the existing system.** That is, **politics no longer offers meaningful opposition or change**, but instead becomes a **technical process of administration, management, and consensus-building within the established order**.

---

### **Key Points:**

#### **1. Politics Has Become Administration**

- Political institutions are no longer arenas of real conflict or transformation—they are **tools for maintaining and managing the system**.
    
- Political discourse is reduced to **technical questions**, not value-driven debates or radical alternatives.
    
- **Bureaucracies, parties, and unions** are all implicated in reproducing the existing order.
    

#### **2. The "Two-Party" System and False Choices**

- Even when choice appears to exist (e.g., in liberal democracies), it's between **variants of the same system**—a “tweedledum and tweedledee” situation.
    
- **Opposition is absorbed** and reshaped to fit within acceptable parameters—what Marcuse calls **“repressive tolerance.”**
    

#### **3. Integration of Opposition**

- **Radical movements, protests, and critiques** are either ignored or **co-opted and neutralized** by the system.
    
- The system’s capacity to **“contain contradictions”** means that resistance often ends up reinforcing the very things it sought to dismantle.
    

#### **4. Depoliticization of the Public**

- Citizens are increasingly **passive, de-politicized, and disengaged**.
    
- Political participation is reduced to **consumer-like behavior**: voting, surveys, symbolic gestures.
    
- The public is led to **identify with the system** (through nationalism, media, consumerism), even when it doesn’t serve their interests.
    

#### **5. Radical Change Becomes Unthinkable**

- Because all visible options are variations of the same logic, **imagination is stifled**.
    
- The very idea of a **qualitatively different society** becomes **“unrealistic,” “utopian,” or “irrational.”**
    

---

### **Marcuse’s Conclusion:**

The political universe is “closed” not because dissent is violently suppressed, but because **society has absorbed opposition by reducing it to manageable, harmless forms.** As a result, **true liberation requires more than just participation in existing institutions—it demands a radical reimagining of what politics could be.**

---

Let me know if you'd like a quote bank or a breakdown of how this connects to modern political culture.

##### You said:

Let's talk about Baudrilliard -- did he have associations with anarchism or Marxism?

###### ChatGPT said:

Jean Baudrillard is a really interesting figure when it comes to this question—because his relationship to **Marxism and anarchism** is **complicated**. He was **influenced by Marxism early on**, but **moved sharply away from it** later. His relationship with anarchism is **more philosophical than political**, but he shares **some deep affinities** with anarchist critique, especially in his **suspicion of structures of power, representation, and reality itself**.

Here’s the breakdown:

---

### **1. Early Influence: Marxism and Post-Marxism**

- **Baudrillard started his career as a Marxist sociologist**—his early works (_The System of Objects_, _The Consumer Society_) are rooted in **Marxian analysis of commodities, alienation, and ideology**.
    
- But **even in his Marxist phase**, he was **more focused on symbolic value and culture** than classical economic analysis.
    
- By the **mid-1970s**, with works like _Symbolic Exchange and Death_, he **rejected Marxism almost entirely**, arguing that:
    
    - Capitalism had moved beyond the realm of production into **the realm of simulation and signs**.
        
    - **Class struggle** and **material exploitation** were still real, but **the system now controls people primarily through signs and images** rather than just economic relations.
        

In other words:  
✅ Early Baudrillard: **Marx-ish**, focusing on commodities and alienation.  
❌ Later Baudrillard: **Post-Marxist**, saying Marxism failed to grasp the full nature of contemporary domination.

---

### **2. Affinity with Anarchism (but not organizational)**

Baudrillard was **never a political anarchist** (he wasn’t organizing or advocating for federations or syndicalism), but he shared **anarchist philosophical tendencies**:

✅ **Deep suspicion of power structures** – Not just the state or capitalism, but **all systems of representation, meaning, and order**.  
✅ **Critique of representation and mediation** – Much like anarchists critique hierarchical mediation between people (e.g., leaders, politicians), Baudrillard critiques **the mediation of reality itself through media, spectacle, and simulation**.  
✅ **Anti-institutional spirit** – He distrusted both **state authority** and **revolutionary vanguards** (like Marxist-Leninists) that sought to **manage** liberation.

Some have called his stance a kind of **"nihilistic anarchism"**—he **wanted to collapse systems of control but didn’t believe in offering blueprints for new structures**. He **mocked both the system and most forms of organized resistance**, seeing them as often caught within the same logic of simulation.

---

### **3. Involvement with 1968 and Aftermath**

- **Baudrillard was shaped by the May 1968 uprisings in France**, where **students and workers launched mass protests** challenging capitalism, bureaucracy, and authoritarianism.
    
- He **supported the spirit of the revolt** but became **skeptical** of how quickly revolt could be absorbed or simulated by the system itself.
    
- This **suspicion of even revolutionary imagery** became a hallmark of his later work.
    

---

### **4. So in Short:**

- **Early Marxist**, later **Post-Marxist skeptic**.
    
- **Not a political anarchist**, but **philosophically anarchistic** in his **hostility to structures of power and meaning**.
    
- Believed **both capitalism and organized revolution risk becoming simulations** unless radically detached from representational systems.
    

---

If you want, I can also sketch a quick **Baudrillard vs. traditional Marxism vs. anarchism** comparison table—it might help to see it visually. Let me know!

##### You said:

Can you dive deeper into his suspicion of revolutionary imagery?

###### ChatGPT said:

Absolutely—this is one of the **most fascinating and difficult aspects of Baudrillard’s thought.** His suspicion of **revolutionary imagery** goes to the heart of his theory of **simulation**: the idea that in modern society, **images and signs no longer represent reality—they replace it.**

Applied to revolution, **Baudrillard warned that the imagery of rebellion itself could become a spectacle**—**a hollow performance** that **reinforces the system instead of overthrowing it**.

---

### **How Baudrillard Saw Revolutionary Imagery:**

#### **1. From Representation to Simulation**

- In the classical world, an image might **represent** a real struggle (e.g., workers on strike).
    
- In late capitalist society (what he calls the era of simulation), the **image becomes detached** from the actual material struggle—it **becomes a product, a symbol, a brand.**
    
- **Revolution is turned into media content**—digestible, packaged, and ultimately harmless.
    
    - E.g., T-shirts with Che Guevara’s face.
        
    - News coverage of protests framed as colorful “events” but stripped of political meaning.
        
    - Hollywood films portraying rebellion (e.g., _V for Vendetta_) offering **aesthetic catharsis without real social change.**
        

---

#### **2. The Revolt Becomes Part of the System**

- Baudrillard argued that **the spectacle of rebellion** could be **absorbed by the system to reproduce itself.**
    
- Instead of suppressing dissent violently (as in old authoritarian systems), **capitalism neutralizes dissent by commodifying it**.
    
    - Radical slogans, images, and even movements are **co-opted**, sold back to people as lifestyle choices, fashion, or entertainment.
        
    - The system thus **maintains control precisely by appearing tolerant of resistance.**
        

---

#### **3. Simulacrum of Resistance**

- Baudrillard introduces the idea of **the simulacrum**—a copy of something that **no longer has any original reality.**
    
- He feared that **contemporary acts of "resistance" often became simulations**:
    
    - They _looked_ revolutionary.
        
    - They _felt_ subversive.
        
    - But they didn’t **challenge the material bases of power**—they were performances **circulating within the system** rather than breaking it.
        

As he put it in _Simulacra and Simulation_ (1981):

> **"Simulation is no longer that of a territory, a referential being, or a substance. It is the generation by models of a real without origin or reality: a hyperreal."**

Applied to revolutionary imagery: **the rebellion becomes hyperreal—it exists only as an image, not a lived political rupture.**

---

### **4. Why Was He So Concerned?**

Baudrillard’s concern wasn't just academic pessimism. He saw this process as:

- **Undermining real change** by offering people **symbolic participation** instead of material action.
    
- **Training people to confuse signs with substance**, leaving them emotionally satisfied but politically disarmed.
    
- **Making true rupture harder** because any emerging radicalism could be **instantly turned into another image, another spectacle, another product.**
    

---

### **In Short:**

Baudrillard was suspicious of revolutionary imagery because he believed that:

- **Modern systems can co-opt and neutralize dissent by transforming it into consumable signs.**
    
- **Even authentic rebellion can be simulated and rendered harmless through media and culture.**
    
- **Liberation must avoid falling into the trap of spectacle—otherwise, it only reinforces the status quo.**
    

---

If you want, I can also share a few **real-world examples** from recent history that fit this model almost too perfectly. Just say the word!

##### You said:

Tell me how you’d describe that concept to a novice

###### ChatGPT said:

Sure—here’s how I’d describe **"sorcerers of atmosphere"** to someone new to Baudrillard’s thought:

---

**In simple terms:**  
**"Sorcerers of atmosphere" are people or systems that create a powerful emotional environment—an illusion—that shapes how we experience reality, without changing anything real underneath.**

They **manipulate feelings, moods, and appearances** so that **everything _feels_ full of meaning or rebellion or depth**, even when **the underlying structures of power and control remain untouched.**

Instead of leading you to question or resist the system, they **distract or pacify you with vibes, spectacle, and sensation.**

---

**Example:**

- A big-budget political protest concert, where you **feel** like part of a revolution—but you go home afterward, nothing about power actually changes, and corporations profit from the event.
    
- A brand selling "authentic rebellion" (think: "Be Different" ads from huge tech companies)—making you **feel** like a rebel by buying a product.
    
- Media coverage of a serious protest that focuses entirely on **the visuals and drama** ("fiery clashes," "colorful banners") rather than the protestors’ demands or the reasons for the uprising.
    

In all these cases, **the "atmosphere" is intense, moving, emotional—but it’s a kind of sorcery**, because it **replaces real change with the _illusion_ of engagement and meaning.**

---

**Baudrillard's warning:**  
The more skillful the **sorcerers of atmosphere** become, the harder it is for people to tell the difference between **true action** and **simulated, hollow performances of change.**

---

Would you also like a short 2–3 sentence version in case you ever want to just explain it casually?

Yeah please

###### ChatGPT said:

Here’s a quick version you can use:

> **"Sorcerers of atmosphere" create powerful moods and feelings that make people _feel_ like something meaningful is happening, even when nothing really changes. They use spectacle and emotion to distract from real power structures, replacing action with the illusion of engagement.**
